  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_371ca0ed-a478-4726-a720-42f33d2fd2ab'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer15531882452016649693">Poe's work was always so dear to me because of two reasons: one of narration and another of a linguistic one. <br/><br/>The narration, story, plot, character, and form, all serve the darkness in his works. You'll see wicked people, sinister intentions, and dark pasts like we would see in our own lives.<br/><br/>And the language he uses is simply magnificent. For someone like me, who happened to find his book in his early stages of learning the English language, although the text was too complicated, it helped me un</span>
  <span id="freeText15531882452016649693" style="display:none">Poe's work was always so dear to me because of two reasons: one of narration and another of a linguistic one. <br /><br />The narration, story, plot, character, and form, all serve the darkness in his works. You'll see wicked people, sinister intentions, and dark pasts like we would see in our own lives.<br /><br />And the language he uses is simply magnificent. For someone like me, who happened to find his book in his early stages of learning the English language, although the text was too complicated, it helped me understand my mania for languages. No matter how fluent you are, this book has lots of words and expressions to teach you. If you have a thing for metaphors, this book will amaze you. And if you're in for rhymes and cadence, you'll be astonished that, even though it's a type of prose, you're reading, but there's certain music to its sentences.<br /><br />I believe, for one who's a learner and a writer, this book should be in his biblical shelf all the time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15531882452016649693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2651250800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2651250800" />
    <a title="livvingitup" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56036469-livvingitup"><img alt="livvingitup" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496410628p2/56036469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2651250800?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="livvingitup" class="user" itemprop="url" name="livvingitup" href="/user/show/56036469-livvingitup">livvingitup</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56036469-livvingitup?shelf=on-the-bookshelf">on-the-bookshelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2651250800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16203858238945841394"><b>Edgar Allen Poe is a literary genius</b><br/><br/>His prose is frightening, chilling and eerily atmospheric. His language choices only further enriched the atmosphere that he constructed with precision and mastery; and the setting were vividly imagined. They were so richly illustrated that I was almost afraid myself to venture into them. Poe is a writer that deserves so much more credit and praise for the gift he had for nothing will <i>feel</i> like this anthology does. <br/><br/>The stories themselves may have initially bee</span>
  <span id="freeText16203858238945841394" style="display:none"><b>Edgar Allen Poe is a literary genius</b><br /><br />His prose is frightening, chilling and eerily atmospheric. His language choices only further enriched the atmosphere that he constructed with precision and mastery; and the setting were vividly imagined. They were so richly illustrated that I was almost afraid myself to venture into them. Poe is a writer that deserves so much more credit and praise for the gift he had for nothing will <i>feel</i> like this anthology does. <br /><br />The stories themselves may have initially been a struggle to completely invest myself in but once I did I was utterly swept away. I was enthralled by the peculiar storylines and nothing could have prepared me each of the endings. They were all completely unique and unexpected, and raised more questions than they answered. I was often left shaken to my core and was left gaping at the final words. Agatha Christie is the only writer who has ever had this effect on me and it was almost as if Death had gifted them the same morbid gift of writing. <br /><br />Overall, Edgar Allen Poe is a brutal writer and wasn't afraid to dabble with sorcery and create stories that should've stayed in the depths of Hell. They were dark and often had me question my own morals and fleeting mortality. Alas, it was darkness I didn't want to be led out of. I wanted to revel with the demons on Poe's mind. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16203858238945841394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3393977055" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3393977055" />
    <a title="blake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33356881-blake"><img alt="blake" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3393977055?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="blake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="blake" href="/user/show/33356881-blake">blake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 0307016137" href="/book/show/6774946-tales-of-edgar-allan-poe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33356881-blake?shelf=reading-all-my-books-project">reading-all-my-books-project</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33356881-blake?shelf=avoiding-ulysses-by-joyce">avoiding-ulysses-by-joyce</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3393977055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16984016125642449821">I mean, it's Poe. What do you want/expect? If you like Poe, this is Poe. If you don't like Poe, this is still Poe. Poe est. Apart from the usual suspects ("Cask", "Heart", "Usher"), some slightly rarer entries, like "The Gold Bug"—which is basically a shaggy dog/cipher story—and much rarer entries like "Berenice", "A Descent into the Maelström" and the "Fight Club" presaging "William Wilson".<br/><br/>"The Gold-Bug" starts out with a typical Poe "hero", a man with an obsession telling his weird story to a</span>
  <span id="freeText16984016125642449821" style="display:none">I mean, it's Poe. What do you want/expect? If you like Poe, this is Poe. If you don't like Poe, this is still Poe. Poe est. Apart from the usual suspects ("Cask", "Heart", "Usher"), some slightly rarer entries, like "The Gold Bug"—which is basically a shaggy dog/cipher story—and much rarer entries like "Berenice", "A Descent into the Maelström" and the "Fight Club" presaging "William Wilson".<br /><br />"The Gold-Bug" starts out with a typical Poe "hero", a man with an obsession telling his weird story to a disbelieving narrator. It gets supernatural...or does it? But the story's conclusion literally walks the reader through a substitution cipher, which seems charmingly quaint in this day-and-age.<br /><br />"Berenice" is the story, essentially, of a sociopath who has an ardent lover (the eponymous Berenice) but only has eyes for her <i>teeth</i>. Add in a little premature burial, and you got yourself a creepy story.<br /><br />"William Wilson" is the story of a dissolute man who's plagued by his doppleganger, the denouement of which was pretty obvious, and basically spelled out from "go", which makes me wonder if it's just this latter year awareness of plot devices that makes it obvious, or maybe this is why the story doesn't make it into the collections very often.<br /><br />"Maelstrom" is another rare entry which reminded me heavily of a scene from one of the <i>Pirates of the Caribbean</i>, which got me to thinking about the impact of CGI on horror. I think practical movie effects had a distinct effect on horror of the '80s and '90s, and I wonder now to what degree authors (and readers) think in terms of what they've seen in modern movies.<br /><br />Idle thoughts as I contemplate the remaining books under "P". I have the complete two volume Poe, which is going to be daunting given the amount of Latin and French, two volumes of Proust (oy), and Pynchon (oy! oy!).<br /><br />The cover of the book makes it look a little like a younger reader's adaptation, but it doesn't appear to be edited in any way. The interior drawings are fairly cheap scribbles.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16984016125642449821" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2482820616" />
    <a title="katja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82373479-katja"><img alt="katja" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594765631p2/82373479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2482820616?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="katja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="katja" href="/user/show/82373479-katja">katja</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2482820616&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2482820616" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3419573878319156047">While reading Edgar Allan Poe's stories I was at the same time frightened and astonished. He is, by all means, the Master of horror and creepiness. <br/><br/>The Masque of the Red Death is a novella in which all the elements mentioned above appear. The novella deals with the time period of the bubonic plague when no one was actually safe. Although there was no escape, a young prince called Prospero thought he could deceive death. He organized a ball in order to forget the misfortune outside of his castle.</span>
  <span id="freeText3419573878319156047" style="display:none">While reading Edgar Allan Poe's stories I was at the same time frightened and astonished. He is, by all means, the Master of horror and creepiness. <br /><br />The Masque of the Red Death is a novella in which all the elements mentioned above appear. The novella deals with the time period of the bubonic plague when no one was actually safe. Although there was no escape, a young prince called Prospero thought he could deceive death. He organized a ball in order to forget the misfortune outside of his castle. Never the less, he was wrong, for no one could avoid death no matter how hard he tried.<br />It is especially interesting to read the detailed description of the rooms in the castle and the magical influence of colours, which symbolically represent life and death. Death is symbolised by the person behind the red masque who dances all the time in all rooms. Everyone is quietly thinking about the unusual masque and the complete horror happens at midnight when death strikes and the guests suddenly start to die. The whole atmosphere is intensed by the interior of the black room where death keeps striking and finally takes Prospero's life as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3419573878319156047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3072373102" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3072373102" />
    <a title="Osama Siddique" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72985691-osama-siddique"><img alt="Osama Siddique" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577733673p2/72985691.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3072373102?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Osama Siddique" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Osama Siddique" href="/user/show/72985691-osama-siddique">Osama Siddique</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72985691-osama-siddique?shelf=horror-supernatural-gothic">horror-supernatural-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72985691-osama-siddique?shelf=american-short-story">american-short-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3072373102" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14360256994313984262">This is a very good selection - in a beautiful bound edition with B &W stylized and highly compelling illustrations by Harry Clarke - that is divided into three sub-genres, a great testament to Poe's remarkable talent and versatility. <br/><br/>(1) Tales of the Grotesque & Arabesque<br/><br/>King Pest - truly conveys a sense of grotesque as two drunk sailors flee a pub and make over to the barricaded part of London ravaged by plague and decomposition. The diction is deliberately ornate and highfalutin, the characte</span>
  <span id="freeText14360256994313984262" style="display:none">This is a very good selection - in a beautiful bound edition with B &amp;W stylized and highly compelling illustrations by Harry Clarke - that is divided into three sub-genres, a great testament to Poe's remarkable talent and versatility. <br /><br />(1) Tales of the Grotesque &amp; Arabesque<br /><br />King Pest - truly conveys a sense of grotesque as two drunk sailors flee a pub and make over to the barricaded part of London ravaged by plague and decomposition. The diction is deliberately ornate and highfalutin, the characters and events exaggerated and unreal, and the overall mood macabre. <br /><br />M.S. found in a Bottle - Unnamed narrator who after his ship capsizes takes refuge on a ghostly ship being sailed by a spectral crew towards the South Pole, driven by a magical Simoom (combination of a hurricane and a sandstorm), where eventually it begins to sink in a vast whirlpool <br /><br />Silence-a fable -- A really fantastical and symbolic tale in a weird and strangely evocative landscape<br /><br />Berenice -- A really morbid story of an obsessive narrator and his paramour, being buried alive, breaking open the grave and surgically removing her teeth in his madness while person still alive - some of the more graphic sections taken out due to public protest. As insight into a mind that is deeply interested in depravity and willing to push the limits<br /><br />William Wilson -- The narrator is obsessed and subsequently pursued by a doppelgänger kind of a character who pursues him as he embarks on a career unscrupulous, debauched and dissipated. To his frustration he finds him to be his nemesis and undoer at every step. Though one gets a sense as the story progresses that it is hard to imagine the doppelgänger as anything but a case of alter-ego or split personality or ravings of an unsettled mind.<br /><br />Ligeia -- Poe at his best in terms of creating phantasmagoria. A spouse of mythical beauty and grace, hailing from mysterious origins and knowledgable in the occult, dies. But as it turns out the successor starts falling ill as well as if through the sinister intervention of her predecessor. But then the corpse seems to be undergoing revivification to ultimately transform into Ligeia - the original beauty. But then, hard to overlook, that all this 'resurrection' is being observed through the unreliable, opium laced vision of the narrator. <br /><br />The assignation -- This sombre and enigmatic tale is set in mysterious Venice. The narrator riding in a gondola in the evening through the dark waters of Venice is witness to a child having fallen in the canal and rescued by a young man who later proves to be a notable, manic and one living in great splendour. There is an enigmatic connection it appears between the beautiful Marchessa whose child fell and the rescuer - both of whom die in the morning as apparently pre-planned - their assignation.<br /><br />The facts in the case of M. Valdemar -- This is a disturbing story about mesmerism arresting the transition between life and death through an experiment conducted on a dying man with his consent. Has some truly memorable moments.<br /><br />(2) Tales of horror and suspense <br /><br />The pit and the pendulum - This is the story I have always closely associated with Poe's characteristic style. The hellish pit with the seemingly bottomless well in Toledo - the Spanish Inquisition - the growing horror as it emerges what the mode of punishment is - and then that stellar building of suspense<br /><br />The fall of the house of usher -- This is not just one of the most well-known Poe story but also a classic Gothic tale in its combination of the psychological, the phantasmic and the atmospheric. Burial alive figures again and the creation of mood has all the standard ingredients - there is madness, loneliness, metaphysical phenomenon, a crumbling and haunted old house, and a dying dynasty. The writing is at times too obtuse and turgid for my taste but it is typical of the genre at that stage.<br /><br />The cask of Amontillado -- This is one of Poe's stories that divulge his fascination with getting buried alive. This time the claustrophobia and sense of menace is provided by a dark and cavernous wine cellar in an unnamed Italian city during a carnival. Revenge for an unspecified reason and the narrative from the revenge seeker's perspective make this a sinister and enigmatic story.<br /><br />A descent into the maelström -- A true masterpiece of Poe's descriptive ability, the coming of the hurricane and the dizzying descent into the thunderous and madly gyrating maelstrom is remarkably powerful and harrowing. Set in the sea off the coast of Norway, apart from horror this story has also been categorized as early science fiction as well as one of Poe's stories of ratiocination. <br /><br />The tell-tale heart -- Another revenge tale - the narrator comes across as neurotic and obsessed and quite possibly mad. A real eerie feel to the story as one encounters the horror of the narrator's action as well as his own sense of dread.<br /><br />The black cat -- Another story with a buried alive theme but not quite, though it does play a role in the plot. Like so many other Poe stories the narrator in unreliable and even unhinged as well as increasingly diabolical in his cruelty to animals and his murderous instincts. Poe captures so well the machinations of depraved minds - the obsession, the paranoia, the capitulation of the capacity to make moral distinctions, the propensity of violence, the sadism.<br /><br />The masque of the red death -- This story is all atmosphere. A dreadful contagion persuades a Prince to seclude himself in a fantastically designed abbey with his entire entourage with the aim to withhold within all of them and withhold without the dreaded Red Death. But Red Death does follow and the descriptions of the encounter are a classic of macabre fiction.<br /><br />(3) Tales of ratiocination<br /><br />The gold-bug -- This underlines why Poe is considered a founder of detective fiction as well. Excavating a hidden treasure is the object of the tale and it is essentially the deconstruction of how a cipher decoded and the treasure found - but Poe's description of the wild country on Sullivan island near Charleston, South Carolina, where it is hidden makes it quiet atmospheric. <br /><br />The murders in the rue morgue -- Starring Poe's French detective Dupin. Two gruesome and violent murders and though cries of victims heard by many who climb up and break into the apartment, no sign of murderer who likely possessed extra-human strength. Much of the story explains Dupin's method of detection and deduction and is like a treatise. An example of stories that introduce an exotic element of the east - an Orangutan in this case. This is a significant piece of literature as it is regarded the first modern detective story that inspired the creation of the likes of Holmes and Poirot.<br /><br />The purloined letter -- Exact thinking and a reasoned train of thought is the intent of this story set in Paris where again the mystery is solved beforehand - of the theft of a letter carrying great significance in the political and diplomatic world - and Poe's detective Dupin then describes the process of solving the crime.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14360256994313984262" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 0307016137" href="/book/show/6774946-tales-of-edgar-allan-poe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41918078-debbie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41918078-debbie?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41918078-debbie?shelf=horror">horror</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2996358118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer460318123220725483">Although this was on my "Want to Read" list for some time, I especially wanted to read this book now after being intrigued by the references to Poe's works (some familiar, some not) in the story THE WIDOW OF PALE HARBOR. Unfortunately, this particular book (of 11 tales) does not include any of Poe's poetry. Edgar Allan Poe is truly the "father of modern horror"! Overall, his stories are very dark and macabre. My favourite in this collection was "The Pit and the Pendulum" - intense! Although some</span>
  <span id="freeText460318123220725483" style="display:none">Although this was on my "Want to Read" list for some time, I especially wanted to read this book now after being intrigued by the references to Poe's works (some familiar, some not) in the story THE WIDOW OF PALE HARBOR. Unfortunately, this particular book (of 11 tales) does not include any of Poe's poetry. Edgar Allan Poe is truly the "father of modern horror"! Overall, his stories are very dark and macabre. My favourite in this collection was "The Pit and the Pendulum" - intense! Although some of the stories were "easy" reads, I did find that some were difficult to follow; however, I did learn a lot of new vocabulary! I noticed that all of the tales in this book have abrupt endings, which were, at times, disappointing. Also, some stories had redundant/pointless sections that rambled on. The simple, monochromatic illustrations by Jerry E. Johnson deliciously added to the mystery of each story. I recommend reading Poe's works, but I highly suggest having a dictionary on hand.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2698838992" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2698838992" />
    <a title="Amy Reade" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8161675-amy-reade"><img alt="Amy Reade" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442975236p2/8161675.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2698838992?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy Reade" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy Reade" href="/user/show/8161675-amy-reade">Amy Reade</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2698838992" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9598304790210787900">This was a collection of some of Poe's better-known works, including The Purloined Letter and The Murders in the Rue Morgue, two of my favorites. It did not include The Raven, though I wish it had. It's interesting to read American fiction from the nineteenth century to see how far readers' preferences have come. If someone tried to write like Poe now, people likely wouldn't take the time to read the book. It was good to sit down and force myself to read slowly and enjoy the stories. Some of the</span>
  <span id="freeText9598304790210787900" style="display:none">This was a collection of some of Poe's better-known works, including The Purloined Letter and The Murders in the Rue Morgue, two of my favorites. It did not include The Raven, though I wish it had. It's interesting to read American fiction from the nineteenth century to see how far readers' preferences have come. If someone tried to write like Poe now, people likely wouldn't take the time to read the book. It was good to sit down and force myself to read slowly and enjoy the stories. Some of them ran a little long, such as The Murders in the Rue Morgue, which begins with a discussion of chess and whist and an endless lecture on the differences in attention, cunning, and intellect displayed by experts in each. But for the most part I enjoyed the collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9598304790210787900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1142971653" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1142971653" />
    <a title="alvaro navas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38240910-alvaro-navas"><img alt="alvaro navas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419440031p2/38240910.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1142971653?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="alvaro navas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="alvaro navas" href="/user/show/38240910-alvaro-navas">alvaro navas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1142971653" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1076314763961627946">Passion, madness, fear. Those three elements are probably the cornerstone of each of the stories Poe made up. <br/>Considering this author a master in gothic short stories, there isn't much left to be said. <br/>Taking for granted that the idea of gothic literature might be considered out of fashioned, the reader experiments a thrilling, shocking atmosphere to the unknown. An apparition that is faded away through the walls of a massive castle, a domestic, innocent cat that chases all of the human mind's f</span>
  <span id="freeText1076314763961627946" style="display:none">Passion, madness, fear. Those three elements are probably the cornerstone of each of the stories Poe made up. <br />Considering this author a master in gothic short stories, there isn't much left to be said. <br />Taking for granted that the idea of gothic literature might be considered out of fashioned, the reader experiments a thrilling, shocking atmosphere to the unknown. An apparition that is faded away through the walls of a massive castle, a domestic, innocent cat that chases all of the human mind's fears, or whatever story his witty mind could invent, none may just leave this current world without having read these collection of stories not to be read during the daylight. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1076314763961627946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

After a while, I came to enjoy each story. <br />While not a quick and easy read, I am glad I read it. Now I have a greater appreciation for Poe.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11310604071218320348">Read this in my quest to round out the list of things I should have read but never did. All the classics are here, some of which I did read in junior high, as well as other, lesser works. The gothic stuff is the best, the science fiction is painful. The mysteries are fine enough, a bit simplistic and too pat. Clearly, though, Poe was an innovator in a number of fields, and much of his work still holds up today.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vA5TPbdLbH33Y8bbudB3XmLqUI63XpJMqsxHEnfbjm+HMRx3gpPQFNva5H6E1qPuIVeCGZbfvRus/YaQf780gg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yZJdBQmDnc+0Ie4QPdFvJ52JnzTA+HDuGH9rUmPJ7QLyrRJPPFshppiYzLUA17uX3jRNo+F5X7keTqrQa61X7w==')})
  }

It presents a detailed imaginative description of how the earth might look wh<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11833249371363802878\" style=\"display:none\">Somnium (Latin for &quot;The Dream&quot;) is a fantasy written between 1620 and 1630 by Johannes Kepler in which a student of Tycho Brahe is transported to the Moon by occult forces. It presents a detailed imaginative description of how the earth might look when viewed from the moon, and is considered the first serious scientific treatise on lunar astronomy. Carl Sagan and Isaac Asimov have referred to it as the first work of science fiction.\n\nSomnium began as a student dissertation in which Kepler defended the Copernican doctrine of the motion of the Earth, suggesting that an observer on the Moon would find the planet\'s movements as clearly visible as the Moon\'s activity is to the Earth\'s inhabitants. Nearly 20 years later, Kepler added the dream framework, and after another decade, he drafted a series of explanatory notes reflecting upon his turbulent career and the stages of his intellectual development. The book was edited by his heirs, including Jacob Bartsch, after Kepler\'s death in 1630, and was published posthumously in 1634.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11833249371363802878\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">About Edgar Allan Poe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1454522972p3/4624490.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">Edgar Allan Poe
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
20,397
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6298700358522696182">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of</span>
  <span id="freeText6298700358522696182" style="display:none">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of essays and book reviews. He is widely acknowledged as the inventor of the modern detective story and an innovator in the science fiction genre, but he made his living as America’s first great literary critic and theoretician. Poe’s reputation today rests primarily on his tales of terror as well as on his haunting lyric poetry.<br /><br />Just as the bizarre characters in Poe’s stories have captured the public imagination so too has Poe himself. He is seen as a morbid, mysterious figure lurking in the shadows of moonlit cemeteries or crumbling castles. This is the Poe of legend. But much of what we know about Poe is wrong, the product of a biography written by one of his enemies in an attempt to defame the author’s name.<br /><br />The real Poe was born to traveling actors in Boston on January 19, 1809. Edgar was the second of three children. His other brother William Henry Leonard Poe would also become a poet before his early death, and Poe’s sister Rosalie Poe would grow up to teach penmanship at a Richmond girls’ school. Within three years of Poe’s birth both of his parents had died, and he was taken in by the wealthy tobacco merchant John Allan and his wife Frances Valentine Allan in Richmond, Virginia while Poe’s siblings went to live with other families. Mr. Allan would rear Poe to be a businessman and a Virginia gentleman, but Poe had dreams of being a writer in emulation of his childhood hero the British poet Lord Byron. Early poetic verses found written in a young Poe’s handwriting on the backs of Allan’s ledger sheets reveal how little interest Poe had in the tobacco business.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_allan_poe">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_al...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6298700358522696182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
